\begin{itemize}

So let's analyze the function g(x) = 3λ - x - sqrt(4 - x^2). For x in [0, √2] (since x_2 ≤ √2). We need g(x) > 0 for all x in [0, √2] to guarantee that Alice can win on turn3 with x_1=0. We need to find the λ where the minimum of g(x) over x in [0, √2] is zero. Let's solve for λ.

We can square or analyze. Note that sqrt(4-x^2) is concave; 3λ - x is linear. The condition for the existence of x such that 3λ - x ≤ sqrt(4-x^2) is equivalent to 3λ ≤ x + sqrt(4-x^2). The maximum of x + sqrt(4-x^2) on [0,2] occurs? Let's compute h(x)=x+sqrt(4-x^2). Derivative: 1 - x/sqrt(4-x^2). Set to zero: sqrt(4-x^2)=x => 4-x^2=x^2 => 2x^2=4 => x=√2≈1.414. At x=√2, h=√2+√2=2√2≈2.828. At x=0, h=0+2=2. At x=2, h=2+0=2. So the maximum is at x=√2, h=2√2. So the minimum possible value of 3λ that ensures the inequality for all x is when 3λ ≥ 2√2? Actually, we need 3λ - x > sqrt(4-x^2) for all x. This is equivalent to 3λ > x+sqrt(4-x^2) for all x. The worst case is the maximum of the RHS, which is 2√2. So if 3λ > 2√2, then for all x, 3λ - x > sqrt(4-x^2) holds. If 3λ = 2√2, then at x=√2, we have 3λ - √2 = 2√2 - √2 = √2, and sqrt(4-2)=√2, so equality. So at λ = (2√2)/3 ≈ 0.9428, we have g(√2)=0. For λ slightly less than that, g(√2)<0, so Bazza can choose x_2=√2, and then 3λ - √2 ≤ sqrt(2), so the interval (sqrt(4-x_2^2), 3λ-x_2] is empty. Then the game continues. So λ=0.9428 is a critical point.

Now, we need to determine if for λ < 2√2/3 ≈0.9428, Alice can still win later, or Bazza can force a win. Let's analyze the range.

We have already that for λ > sqrt(2) ≈1.414, Alice can win on turn1 immediately. For λ between 1 and sqrt(2), we saw she can win on turn3 (since 3λ > 2√2? For λ=1, 3λ=3 >2.828, so yes). For instance, she could choose x_1 = something, then on turn3, she might choose x_3, etc.

We need to characterize the set of λ where Alice has a winning strategy. Similarly, for Bazza to have a winning strategy.

This is reminiscent of a game where players alternately allocate a budget to increase a certain quantity, and the threshold is determined by a "critical" sequence. Possibly the game reduces to a "potential" function like the maximum possible sum of squares after a certain number of moves given the sum constraints.

Another perspective: The game can be viewed as a "fair" game where each player can choose numbers to increase either the sum or the sum of squares, but they both affect both. There might be a known result: the game is a draw for λ ≤ 1? But we just found that for λ=1, Alice wins. So that's not.

Let's test λ=0.9 numerically with some strategies.

Suppose λ=0.9. Alice wants to win. She can try to make T exceed 4 at some odd turn. Let's consider turn3. Before turn3, we have x_1 and x_2. She can choose x_1. Let's see if she can force a win on turn3. Condition: 3λ - x_1 - x_2 > sqrt(4 - x_2^2). She can choose x_1. Bazza will choose x_2 to try to avoid that. Alice can adapt x_1. She might choose x_1 to be something positive, which increases T_1 and also uses up her budget, reducing the allowed x_3. So she needs to balance.

Perhaps a better strategy is to win later, on turn5 or turn7. Let's think about the general game.

Define after each even turn, the state is (S, T). At odd turn, Alice can choose x to increase T, and at even turn, Bazza can choose x to increase S. The constraints are:

- On odd turn n=2k-1: S_{2k-2} + x_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1) and T_{2k-1} = T_{2k-2} + x_{2k-1}^2.
- On even turn n=2k: T_{2k-1} + x_{2k}^2 ≤ 2k and S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + x_{2k}.

So the game is a kind of "resource allocation" where each player can decide how much to invest in increasing the sum (for Bazza) or the sum of squares (for Alice) but both affect the other.

One approach: Use "strategy stealing" or "mirroring" ideas. Perhaps we can find an invariant that shows that if λ is less than some value, Bazza can always win, and if λ is greater than that value, Alice can always win. We already have a threshold: For λ > sqrt(2), Alice wins immediately. But we also have that for λ = 1, Alice wins, and for λ = 0.9428, Alice might still win? Let's check λ=0.94. Then 3λ=2.82, 2√2≈2.828. So 3λ is slightly less than 2√2. At x_2=√2≈1.414, we have sqrt(4-2)=√2≈1.414, and 3λ - x_2 = 2.82-1.414=1.406, which is less than 1.414. So if Bazza chooses x_2=√2, then the allowed x_3 is up to 1.406, but needed x_3 > 1.414, so impossible. So Alice cannot win on turn3 if Bazza plays x_2=√2. So she must try to win later.

But can Bazza always choose x_2=√2? He must respect T_2 ≤2, which holds because (√2)^2=2. So yes, he can choose x_2=√2. If he does that, then after his move, S_2 = x_1 + √2, T_2=2. Then Alice's turn3: she must choose x_3 ≤ 3λ - S_2. If she chooses x_1=0, then S_2=√2≈1.414, so allowed x_3 ≤ 3λ - 1.414. For λ=0.94, 3λ=2.82, so x_3 ≤ 1.406. She wants T_3 > 4? Actually, to win on turn4, she would need T_3 >4. But T_3 = T_2 + x_3^2 = 2 + x_3^2. So she needs x_3^2 >2, i.e., x_3 >1.414. Since she can only pick up to 1.406, she cannot achieve that. So she cannot win on turn4. She could try to win on turn5 (odd turn 5). That would require T_5 > 6? Actually, the next even turn after turn5 is turn6, with bound 6. So she needs T_5 >6. That is, after her move on turn5, T must exceed 6. But she also has constraints on S.

Let's continue.

After turn3, Alice chooses x_3, which also affects S_3 and T_3. Then Bazza on turn4 chooses x_4, which must satisfy T_4 ≤4. Since T_3 = 2 + x_3^2, and x_3 ≤ 1.406, so x_3^2 ≤ 1.977, so T_3 ≤ 3.977. So he can only add a tiny x_4. Then S_4 = S_3 + x_4. S_3 = x_1 + x_2 + x_3 = 0+1.414+ x_3 ≈1.414+ x_3. With x_3 up to 1.406, S_3 could be up to 2.82. Then x_4 is small, so S_4 ≈2.82. Then Alice's turn5: she must choose x_5 such that S_5 = S_4 + x_5 ≤ 5λ = 4.7? Wait, λ=0.94, so 5λ=4.7. She can choose x_5 up to 4.7 - S_4. If S_4≈2.82, then allowed x_5 ≤1.88. She wants T_5 = T_4 + x_5^2 > 6 (since next even turn is 6). T_4 = T_3 + x_4^2 ≤4 (by Bazza's choice). Actually, Bazza will choose x_4 to keep T_4 as low as possible? He wants to survive, but he also wants to win. He will try to make S large later. But for now, he might choose x_4=0 to keep T_4 = T_3, which is at most 3.977. Then T_5 = T_3 + x_5^2. To exceed 6, she needs x_5^2 > 6 - T_3. If T_3 is close to 4, then x_5^2 >2, so x_5 >1.414. But she is allowed x_5 up to 1.88, so she could choose x_5=1.5, then T_5≈4+2.25=6.25>6. Then T_5= T_3 + x_5^2 = 2+1.977 +2.25 = 6.227? Actually, T_3=2+x_3^2=2+1.977=3.977, plus 2.25 =6.227>6. So Bazza loses on turn6. So for λ=0.94, Alice can win by choosing x_1=0, x_3=1.406 (max allowed), then x_5=1.5. But is this allowed? We need to ensure that her choice x_3 respects S_3 ≤3λ=2.82. With x_3=1.406, S_3=1.414+1.406=2.82, okay. Then on turn4, Bazza must choose x_4 such that T_4 ≤4. He can choose x_4=0, then T_4=3.977. Thus, for λ=0.94, Alice can win. So the threshold where Alice can win might be lower than 0.9428. Let's find the exact λ where Alice can guarantee a win.

We need to consider the game in general. Alice can try to win by playing her moves on odd turns to increase T, and Bazza can try to play his moves to increase S. The key is to find a "critical" sequence where Alice can force T to exceed the next even bound, given that Bazza can respond to try to prevent it.

We can think of the game as a kind of "race" where each player tries to push the other's quantity beyond the line. The constraints are linear in n.

We need to determine all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy. Since she can always choose 0 on her turns, the only way to force a win is to make a move that makes T exceed the next even bound. So she needs to eventually find a turn where she can choose x such that T_current + x^2 > next_even_bound, while also respecting S_current + x ≤ λ*odd_bound. She can also influence S_current by her previous moves and Bazza's moves.

We can analyze the game using a "potential" function. Let's denote after each even turn, the state (S, T) with S = sum after even turn, T = sum of squares after even turn, and the next odd turn is 2k+1 with bound λ(2k+1). Then Alice on turn 2k+1 can choose x such that S + x ≤ λ(2k+1). She wants to make T + x^2 > 2k+2? Actually, the next even turn is 2k+2, with bound 2k+2. So she wants to make T + x^2 > 2k+2 to cause Bazza to lose on his turn. So she needs x^2 > 2k+2 - T. So she needs to have a budget to choose such x.

Thus, for each cycle, the condition for Alice to win at her turn is that the maximum allowed x (call X_max = λ(2k+1) - S) is greater than sqrt(2k+2 - T). So we need λ(2k+1) - S > sqrt(2k+2 - T). This is a condition that depends on the state (S, T) before her move.

If she cannot win immediately, she might choose x to try to set up a win later. She could also try to make T large but keep S small so that on her next turn, she has a large budget.

Similarly, Bazza on even turn wants to make S large enough to cause Alice to lose on her next odd turn. He needs S_current + x > λ(2k+1) and x^2 ≤ 2k - T_current? Actually, on turn 2k, before his move, we have state (S, T) after Alice's move on turn 2k-1. Then he can choose x such that T + x^2 ≤ 2k. He wants S + x > λ(2k+1) (since Alice's next turn is 2k+1, with bound λ(2k+1)). So condition: there exists x in [0, sqrt(2k - T)] such that x > λ(2k+1) - S. So need sqrt(2k - T) > λ(2k+1) - S.

So both players have a similar condition.

Now, the game is symmetric in a sense but with different constants: the sum constraints involve λ, while the sum of squares constraints involve 1.

We need to find the λ for which Alice has a winning strategy. Since she can always play 0, but Bazza can also play 0, the outcome can be drawn. However, if one player can force a win, then the other cannot avoid loss. So we need to see if there is a threshold.

From our analysis, for λ > sqrt(2) ≈1.414, Alice wins immediately on turn1. For λ between 1 and sqrt(2), she can win on turn3 (since we can set x_1=0 and then win on turn3). But for λ slightly less than 1, we need to check.

Let's test λ=0.9. We'll try to see if Alice can still win. We need to find a strategy.

Suppose Alice tries to win on turn3. She can choose x_1=0. Then condition for her to win on turn3 is: 3λ - x_2 > sqrt(4 - x_2^2). For λ=0.9, 3λ=2.7. The function h(x)=x+sqrt(4-x^2) has maximum 2√2≈2.828 at x=√2. So there exists x_2 such that 2.7 ≤ x+sqrt(4-x^2) for some x. For instance, at x=√2, h=2.828>2.7, so inequality fails. Bazza can choose x_2=√2, then 2.7 - √2 ≈1.286 < sqrt(2)≈1.414? Actually, sqrt(4-2)=√2≈1.414, and 2.7-1.414=1.286, so he can set x_2=√2, and then allowed x_3 ≤1.286, needed >1.414, so no win on turn3. So she cannot win on turn3. She might try to win on turn5. That requires building up T over two cycles.

Thus, even if Bazza tries to maximize T at each turn, Alice seems to be able to eventually win by using the budget on odd turns to increase T, and then on her later turns she can add large x because the bound grows linearly. The key is that she can always, on her turn, choose x as large as possible (i.e., up to the bound) to increase T, and then on the next even turn, Bazza cannot add too much because T is already high. Then on her next turn, she can again add large x. The pattern might be that for any λ less than some threshold, Alice can still win by playing the maximum allowed each turn, and the threshold might be where she can't make T exceed the bound because the sum constraint is too tight.

Thus, for λ=0.9, Alice can win by not playing the maximum on turn3, but instead playing 0. This is a clever strategy. So the threshold where Bazza can force a draw is lower than 0.9. Let's test λ=0.8 with this approach.

λ=0.8. Turn1: Alice plays 0, S1=0, T1=0. Turn2: Bazza plays x2. He can play up to √2=1.414. He will try to survive. He might play x2=1.414, then S2=1.414, T2=2. Turn3: Alice plays x3. Let's analyze the recurrence in general.

We can think of the game as a kind of "battle" where both players try to force the opponent's quantity above the line. The critical λ0 is where the "worst-case" for Alice is that Bazza can always respond to keep S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1) and T_{2k}=2k, and also keep the inequality that prevents her from winning. Let's derive the recurrence for the state after Bazza's move.

Let’s denote a_k = S_{2k} and b_k = T_{2k} after Bazza's move on turn 2k. We have b_k = 2k (by his strategy). Then Alice on turn 2k+1 chooses x_{2k+1} such that S_{2k+1} = a_k + x_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1). She will choose some x. Then T_{2k+1} = b_k + x_{2k+1}^2 = 2k + x^2. Then Bazza on turn 2k+2 chooses x_{2k+2} such that T_{2k+2} = 2k+2. So x_{2k+2}^2 = 2k+2 - (2k + x^2) = 2 - x^2. So x_{2k+2} = √(2 - x^2) (assuming 2 - x^2 ≥ 0). Then S_{2k+2} = S_{2k+1} + x_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) - (a_k + x) + (a_k + x) + √(2 - x^2)? Wait, careful: S_{2k+1} = a_k + x, and S_{2k+2} = S_{2k+1} + √(2 - x^2) = a_k + x + √(2 - x^2). So a_{k+1} = a_k + x + √(2 - x^2). Then the recurrence for a_k is:

a_{k+1} = a_k + x + √(2 - x^2), where x = x_{2k+1} ∈ [0, λ(2k+1)-a_k].

Alice chooses x to try to win eventually. She wants to make T_{2k+1} > 2k+2, i.e., 2k + x^2 > 2k+2 => x^2 > 2 => x > √2. So if she can choose x > √2, she wins immediately on the next even turn. So she will try to choose x as large as possible, but the allowed maximum is λ(2k+1)-a_k. So she can win at her turn if λ(2k+1)-a_k > √2. That is, a_k < λ(2k+1)-√2.

If this condition holds for some k, then she can win. So the question is: can Bazza always keep a_k such that a_k ≥ λ(2k+1)-√2 for all k, thereby preventing her from winning? If he can, then the game is drawn (or he might win by making S exceed her bound). But he also needs to ensure that S does not exceed λ n at odd turns, which is already satisfied by her choice. He also needs to ensure that his x_{2k+2} is within the allowed range: he needs √(2 - x^2) ≤ sqrt(2k+2 - T_{2k+1})? Actually, he must choose x_{2k+2} such that T_{2k+2} ≤ 2k+2, and we set it exactly to 2k+2 by choosing x_{2k+2}=√(2 - x^2). This is allowed only if 2 - x^2 ≥ 0, i.e., x ≤ √2. But if she chooses x > √2, he loses immediately. So if she can choose x > √2, she wins. So if she cannot choose x > √2 because a_k ≥ λ(2k+1)-√2, then she is forced to choose x ≤ √2. Then Bazza can respond with x_{2k+2}=√(2 - x^2) (which is defined because x ≤ √2). Then the recurrence for a_k is as above.

Now, Bazza wants to survive indefinitely. He can choose his moves to try to keep a_k large enough. But note that a_k evolves as a_{k+1} = a_k + x + √(2 - x^2). Since x is chosen by Alice, and she can choose any x in [0, λ(2k+1)-a_k], but she wants to eventually make x > √2 if possible. So she will choose x as large as possible, i.e., x = λ(2k+1)-a_k. Because that maximizes x and also minimizes √(2 - x^2). But note that if she chooses x = λ(2k+1)-a_k, then that is the maximum allowed. Then the recurrence becomes:

a_{k+1} = a_k + (λ(2k+1)-a_k) + √(2 - (λ(2k+1)-a_k)^2) = λ(2k+1) + √(2 - (λ(2k+1)-a_k)^2).

Thus, a_{k+1} = λ(2k+1) + √(2 - (λ(2k+1)-a_k)^2). This is a recurrence that depends on a_k. We need to see if this recurrence allows Bazza to keep a_k ≥ λ(2k+1)-√2 for all k. If so, then Alice never gets to choose x > √2. But also, we need to check if at some point, she might deviate to a smaller x to set up a win later? That seems unlikely because making x smaller reduces T, making it harder to exceed the bound later. So the optimal for her is to maximize x. So if the recurrence with max x leads to a_k always being large enough, then she cannot win.

Thus, the critical condition is that there exists a sequence a_k such that a_k ∈ [λ(2k+1)-√2, λ(2k+1)] (since she can't win if a_k is at least λ(2k+1)-√2). To win on turn5, she needs d2 > √2, i.e., 5λ - (√2 + x3 + √(2 - x3^2)) > √2 => 5λ > 2√2 + x3 + √(2 - x3^2). The RHS is maximized? For x3 in [0, √2], the function g(x3)=x3+√(2-x3^2) has maximum at x3=√2, giving 2√2. So RHS max is 2√2+2√2=4√2≈5.656. So 5λ > 4√2 => λ > 4√2/5≈1.131, which is not possible for λ<0.85. So she cannot win on turn5 by playing positive x3. Could she win on turn7? Possibly if d3 > √2. But the recurrence becomes more complex. It seems that the best chance for Alice is to minimize the sum S2, i.e., play 0 on turn1, to maximize d1. Then on turn3, she also wants to minimize S3? She wants to keep S3 small to allow larger budget later. Playing 0 is best. So the optimal strategy for her is to always play 0 on her turns, except possibly when she can make T exceed the bound? Actually, she might need to play a positive x on her turn to increase T, but that also increases S, which reduces future budgets. The trade-off is that she wants to increase T enough to win, but also wants to keep S small to allow large x later. In the recurrence, she always chooses the maximum allowed x, which is d_k, which is the largest possible. That leads to the recurrence d_{k+1}=2λ-√(2-d_k^2). This is the best she can do to increase T? Actually, if she chooses x = d_k, then T_{2k+1} = 2k + d_k^2, which is the largest possible given her budget. So that gives her the best chance to win on the next even turn if d_k > √2. So the recurrence is the optimal play for Alice.

Thus, the outcome depends on the dynamics of the recurrence.

We need to find for which λ the sequence d_k defined by d_{k+1} = 2λ - √(2 - d_k^2) (with d_1 = 3λ - √2) eventually becomes > √2. If it does, Alice can win; if it stays ≤ √2 for all k, then Bazza can survive indefinitely. Also, if d_k becomes negative, then Bazza wins. So we need to determine the set of λ for which the sequence does not stay ≤ √2, i.e., there exists k such that d_k > √2.

We can analyze the fixed points. Let d be a fixed point: d = 2λ - √(2-d^2). Then √(2-d^2) = 2λ - d. Squaring: 2-d^2 = (2λ-d)^2 = 4λ^2 -4λd + d^2. So 2-d^2 = 4λ^2 -4λd + d^2 => 2 = 4λ^2 -4λd + 2d^2 => divide 2: 1 = 2λ^2 -2λd + d^2 => d^2 -2λd + 2λ^2 -1 =0 => d = λ ± √(λ^2 - (2λ^2-1))? Actually, solve: d = [2λ ± √(4λ^2 -4(2λ^2-1))]/2 = [2λ ± √(4λ^2 -8λ^2+4)]/2 = [2λ ± √(4-4λ^2)]/2 = λ ± √(1-λ^2). So the fixed points are d = λ ± √(1-λ^2). Note that these are valid only if λ ≤ 1 (since √(1-λ^2) real). Also, we need d ∈ [0, √2]. For λ ≤ 1, λ ± √(1-λ^2) are between 0 and 1? Actually, when λ=0, d = ±1, so d=1 (positive) is fixed point. When λ=1, d = 1 ± 0 = 1. So d=1 is fixed point. Also, note that d = λ + √(1-λ^2) ≥ λ ≥ 0, and d ≤ 1+0=1? Actually, λ+√(1-λ^2) ≤ 1? For λ=0, it's 1; for λ=0.5, it's 0.5+√0.75=0.5+0.866=1.366>1. So it can exceed 1. Check: λ=0.5 gives 1.366, which is >1. But λ=0.5, then √(1-λ^2)=√0.75=0.866, sum=1.366. That is a fixed point? Let's check if it satisfies d = 2λ - √(2-d^2). For λ=0.5, 2λ=1, RHS = 1 - √(2-1.866)=1-√0.134=1-0.366=0.634, not 1.366. So something is off. Because the fixed point equation we derived is d = 2λ - √(2-d^2). But we also derived d = λ ± √(1-λ^2) from squaring. However, that squaring might have introduced extraneous solutions because the square root is defined as nonnegative. Actually, from d = 2λ - √(2-d^2), we have √(2-d^2) = 2λ - d, which requires 2λ - d ≥ 0. So the fixed point must satisfy d ≤ 2λ. So the positive solution from the quadratic might not satisfy this inequality. Let's solve correctly.

We have d = 2λ - √(2-d^2). Then √(2-d^2) = 2λ - d. For this to be valid, we need 2λ - d ≥ 0. Squaring gives 2-d^2 = (2λ-d)^2 = 4λ^2 -4λd + d^2. So the two potential fixed points are d = λ + √(1-λ^2) and d = λ - √(1-λ^2). Now, we also need the inequality 2λ - d ≥ 0. For d = λ + √(1-λ^2), 2λ - d = 2λ - λ - √(1-λ^2) = λ - √(1-λ^2). We had: After Bazza's move on turn 2k, state (S_{2k}, T_{2k}) with T_{2k}=2k. Then Alice on turn 2k+1 chooses x_{2k+1}. She will choose x = d_k, where d_k = λ(2k+1)-S_{2k}. So if she chooses x3=0, then she is not playing the maximum x3; she is playing a smaller x. That changes the state. So our recurrence assumes she plays the maximum allowed x each turn, which is d_k. That may not be optimal in the sense that she might want to play a smaller x to keep S low, but that reduces T, making it harder to win later. However, in the case λ=0.85, we found that by playing x3=0 (not max), she could win on turn5 because d2 = 5λ - S4 becomes large enough. So the optimal strategy for her is not necessarily to always play the maximum; she might want to play a smaller x on her turn to keep the sum S small, so that on the next odd turn, she has a larger budget. But note that playing a smaller x reduces T, which makes it harder to win later. So there is a trade-off.

Thus, we need to consider the game more generally: Alice can choose any x on her turns, subject to S ≤ λ n. She wants to eventually make T exceed the bound at some even turn. Similarly, Bazza can choose any x on his turns, subject to T ≤ n. He wants to make S exceed the bound at some odd turn.

This is a kind of "alternating" game where each player can decide how much to invest in increasing the sum or sum of squares. The thresholds are determined by the "critical" sequence where both players use optimal strategies.

I think the standard solution to such problems involves analyzing the "potential" function S_n^2 - T_n, or something similar, and showing that if λ > 1, Alice wins; if λ < 1, Bazza wins; and at λ = 1, the game is a draw. But our analysis suggests that for λ=0.85, Alice can win, and for λ=0.84, she cannot. So λ=0.85 is > 0.8485, and we found that at λ=0.85, she can win with x1=0, x3=0, x5=1.422. But the problem statement: "Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy." It doesn't ask for draws, but they are part of the classification. Usually, the answer is: Alice wins for λ > 1, Bazza wins for λ < 1, and λ = 1 is a draw. But our calculations show that the thresholds are different. Let's double-check the problem: "Alice and Bazza are playing the inekoalaty game, a two-player game whose rules depend on a positive real number λ which is known to both players. On the nth turn of the game (starting with n=1) the following happens: - If n is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that x_1 + x_2 + ... + x_n ≤ λ n. - If n is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that x_1^2 + x_2^2 + ... + x_n^2 ≤ n. If a player cannot choose a suitable x_n, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players. Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy."

I suspect the intended answer is: Alice wins if λ > 1, Bazza wins if λ < 1, and the game is a draw if λ = 1. But our analysis suggests that at λ=0.8, which is less than 1, Alice can win? Now, we also need to consider the possibility that she might win by a different initial move, e.g., playing x1>0. But that would make S2 larger, reducing d1. So not beneficial. So the condition is as derived.

Thus, we have:

- Bazza wins if λ < √2/2.
- Alice wins if λ > (8√2+3)/17.
- For √2/2 ≤ λ ≤ (8√2+3)/17, the game is a draw.

Now, we need to ensure that for λ exactly √2/2, Bazza cannot win (since the inequality fails). And for λ exactly (8√2+3)/17, Alice cannot win (since d1 = d_+). So draws.

Now, the problem asks: "Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy." So we need to state the intervals. We'll present the solution as:

- Alice has a winning strategy if and only if λ > (8√2+3)/17.
- Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if λ < √2/2.

And note that for λ in [√2/2, (8√2+3)/17], neither player has a winning strategy, so the game is a draw.

Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof. Let's write it step by step.

First, define the game. Let’s denote the state after each move.

We'll prove the two intervals.

Proof for Bazza's win:

Assume λ < √2/2. We'll show that Bazza has a winning strategy.

Strategy: On his first turn (n=2), he chooses x_2 = √2. Then on each subsequent even turn, he chooses the maximum possible x such that the sum of squares does not exceed n, i.e., x_{2k} = √(2k - T_{2k-1}), where T_{2k-1} is the sum of squares after Alice's move on turn 2k-1. This is always feasible because if T_{2k-1} > 2k, then Alice would have won on her previous turn (since Bazza cannot move), but we can argue that Alice will not make that move because it would cause her to lose immediately? Actually, we need to show that the strategy is legal. We'll prove by induction that after his move on turn 2k, we have T_{2k} = 2k and S_{2k} ≥ k√2. Base: k=1, S_2 = x_1 + √2 ≥ √2 = 1·√2. Induction: Suppose S_{2k-2} ≥ (k-1)√2 and T_{2k-2}=2k-2. After Alice's move on turn 2k-1, we have T_{2k-1}=T_{2k-2}+x_{2k-1}^2 = 2k-2 + x_{2k-1}^2. Since she must respect T_{2k-1} ≤ 2k (otherwise Bazza would lose on his turn), we have x_{2k-1}^2 ≤ 2, so x_{2k-1} ≤ √2. Then Bazza on turn 2k chooses x_{2k} = √(2k - T_{2k-1}) = √(2k - (2k-2 + x_{2k-1}^2)) = √(2 - x_{2k-1}^2). Then S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + x_{2k} = (S_{2k-2} + x_{2k-1}) + √(2 - x_{2k-1}^2). Since S_{2k-2} ≥ (k-1)√2 and the function f(x)=x+√(2-x^2) ≥ √2 for all x in [0, √2], we have S_{2k} ≥ (k-1)√2 + √2 = k√2. Also, T_{2k}=T_{2k-1}+x_{2k}^2 = (2k-2 + x_{2k-1}^2) + (2 - x_{2k-1}^2) = 2k. So the induction holds.

Thus, after each even turn, Bazza has T_{2k}=2k and S_{2k} ≥ k√2.

Now, Alice loses on turn 2k+1 if S_{2k} > λ(2k+1). Since S_{2k} ≥ k√2, it suffices to show that there exists some k such that k√2 > λ(2k+1). This inequality is equivalent to λ < k√2/(2k+1). Since the function g(k)=k√2/(2k+1) is increasing and tends to √2/2 as k→∞, we have that if λ < √2/2, then for sufficiently large k, we have λ < g(k). In fact, choose k such that k√2 > λ(2k+1). This is possible because the limit of g(k) is √2/2, and λ is less than that limit. Thus, there exists some k for which Alice cannot move on turn 2k+1. Therefore, Bazza wins.

This proves that for λ < √2/2, Bazza has a winning strategy.

Now, proof for Alice's win:

Assume λ > (8√2+3)/17. We'll show that Alice has a winning strategy.

Strategy: On her first turn, she chooses x_1 = 0. Then on each odd turn, she will choose the maximum possible x (subject to the sum constraint) and we will show that eventually T exceeds the bound on an even turn.

Define the sequence d_k as the budget for Alice on her odd turns. With x_1=0, we have S_2 = x_1 + x_2. Bazza's optimal play is to set T_2=2, so x_2 = √2. Then S_2 = √2. Then the budget on turn 3 is d_1 = 3λ - √2. But she will set x_3 = 0 (since playing a positive x would reduce future budgets). So after turn 3, S_3 = √2, T_3 = 2. Then Bazza on turn 4 sets T_4=4, so x_4 = √2, S_4 = 2√2. Then the budget on turn 5 is d_1 = 5λ - 2√2. She sets x_5 = d_1. Then T_5 = T_4 + d_1^2 = 4 + d_1^2. Then Bazza on turn 6 sets x_6 = √(6 - T_5) = √(2 - d_1^2). Then S_6 = S_5 + x_6 = 5λ + √(2 - d_1^2). Then the budget on turn 7 is d_2 = 7λ - S_6 = 2λ - √(2 - d_1^2). She sets x_7 = d_2. Then T_7 = 6 + d_2^2. Then Bazza on turn 8 sets x_8 = √(8 - T_7) = √(2 - d_2^2). Then S_8 = S_7 + x_8 = 7λ + √(2 - d_2^2). Then budget on turn 9 is d_3 = 9λ - S_8 = 2λ - √(2 - d_2^2). And so on.

Thus, we have the recurrence: d_{k+1} = 2λ - √(2 - d_k^2), with initial d_1 = 5λ - 2√2.

We need to show that there exists some k such that d_k > √2. Then on her turn 2k+1, she chooses x = d_k > √2, and then T_{2k+1} = 2k + d_k^2 > 2k+2, so Bazza cannot move on his next turn (turn 2k+2). Thus, she wins.

Now, we analyze the recurrence. Define f(d)=2λ-√(2-d^2). f is increasing on [0, √2] (since derivative f'(d)=d/√(2-d^2)≥0). The fixed points satisfy d = f(d) => d = 2λ-√(2-d^2) => √(2-d^2)=2λ-d. Squaring: 2-d^2 = 4λ^2 -4λ d + d^2 => 2 = 4λ^2 -4λ d + 2d^2 => 1 = 2λ^2 -2λ d + d^2 => d^2 -2λ d + 2λ^2 -1=0 => d = λ ± √(1-λ^2). For λ > √2/2, the fixed point d_+ = λ+√(1-λ^2) is less than √2. Also, note that f(d) > d for d < d_+ and f(d) < d for d > d_+? Actually, since f is increasing and f(d_+)=d_+, for d < d_+, we have f(d) > d (because if d is slightly less than d_+, f(d) is less than f(d_+)=d_+, but we need to compare f(d) and d. Since f is convex? Let's compute at d=0: f(0)=2λ-√2. For λ > √2/2, 2λ-√2 >0, so f(0)>0. So f(d) > d for d small. The fixed point d_+ is attracting from below? Actually, if d < d_+, then f(d) > d, so the sequence increases towards d_+. If d > d_+, then f(d) < d? Check: at d=√2, f(√2)=2λ. Since λ > √2/2, 2λ > √2? For λ=0.85, 2λ=1.7>1.414, so f(√2) > √2. So f(d) > d at d=√2. That suggests that for d > d_+, f(d) > d as well. But at d=1.38, which is just above d_+ for λ=0.85 (d_+=1.377), we had f(1.38)=1.391>1.38, so f(d) > d. So the fixed point d_+ is actually repelling from both sides? That would mean that if the initial d_1 is less than d_+, the sequence increases towards d_+ but never surpasses it; if d_1 is greater than d_+, the sequence increases further, eventually surpassing √2. So the condition for Alice to win is that d_1 > d_+. Because then the sequence is increasing and will exceed √2 at some point. If d_1 = d_+, the sequence stays at d_+, which is less than √2. If d_1 < d_+, the sequence converges to d_+ from below, never exceeding √2. Thus, Alice wins if and only if d_1 > d_+. So we need to show that λ > (8√2+3)/17 implies d_1 > d_+.

Now, compute d_1 = 5λ - 2√2, d_+ = λ + √(1-λ^2). The inequality d_1 > d_+ is equivalent to 5λ - 2√2 > λ + √(1-λ^2) => 4λ - 2√2 > √(1-λ^2). Square both sides (both sides are nonnegative for λ > √2/2? Check: 4λ-2√2 >0 if λ > √2/2, which holds). So (4λ-2√2)^2 > 1-λ^2 => 16λ^2 -16√2 λ +8 > 1-λ^2 => 17λ^2 -16√2 λ +7 > 0 => The quadratic has roots (16√2 ± √(512-476))/(34) = (16√2 ± 6)/34 = (8√2 ± 3)/17. The quadratic is positive for λ < (8√2-3)/17 or λ > (8√2+3)/17. Since λ > (8√2+3)/17, the inequality holds. Therefore, d_1 > d_+.

Thus, for λ > (8√2+3)/17, we have d_1 > d_+, so the sequence d_k increases and will eventually exceed √2. In fact, we can prove by induction that d_k is increasing and d_k > d_+ for all k. Since d_{k+1} = 2λ - √(2-d_k^2) and d_k > d_+, we have d_{k+1} > f(d_+) = d_+. So the sequence stays above d_+. Moreover, since f(d) > d for d > d_+, the sequence is strictly increasing. Since it is bounded above by √2? Actually, if d_k < √2, then f(d_k) < f(√2)=2λ. But 2λ may be > √2. However, as the sequence increases, it may approach √2. But we need to show that it eventually exceeds √2. Since the sequence is strictly increasing and cannot exceed √2 indefinitely? Actually, if d_k < √2, then f(d_k) is defined, but it could be > √2. In that case, the sequence jumps above √2. So the key is that the fixed point d_+ is less than √2, and for d > d_+, f(d) > d. So if d_1 > d_+, the sequence is increasing. Since it is increasing and bounded above by √2? But f(d) can exceed √2. In fact, if d is close enough to √2, f(d) > √2. So the sequence will eventually exceed √2. More formally, we can prove that there exists k such that d_k > √2. Suppose not, i.e., d_k ≤ √2 for all k. Then since d_k is increasing, it converges to some limit L ≤ √2. Then L must satisfy L = f(L), so L = d_+ (since d_+ is the only fixed point in [0, √2]? Actually, there are two fixed points: d_- and d_+. But d_- is attracting from below, and d_+ is repelling from below. Since the sequence is increasing and starts above d_+, it cannot converge to d_- (which is less than d_+). So the limit must be d_+. But d_+ < √2, so the sequence converges to d_+, which is less than √2. However, we have that f(d) > d for d > d_+. So if d_k > d_+, then d_{k+1} > d_k, so the sequence is strictly increasing. Then it cannot converge to d_+ because it is strictly increasing and d_+ is a fixed point; if d_k > d_+, then d_{k+1} > d_k, so the sequence moves away from d_+. The only way to converge to d_+ is if the sequence starts below d_+ and increases towards it. But here it starts above, so it cannot converge to d_+. Therefore, the assumption that d_k ≤ √2 for all k leads to a contradiction. Hence, there must be some k such that d_k > √2. This completes the proof that Alice wins.

Thus, we have:

- Bazza wins if λ < √2/2.
- Alice wins if λ > (8√2+3)/17.
- For √2/2 ≤ λ ≤ (8√2+3)/17, the game is a draw.

Now, we need to present this in a clean solution.

Let's write the final answer.

We'll output in the required XML format.</think><solution>
We analyse the game by considering the state after each turn.  
Let \(S_n = x_1+\cdots+x_n\) and \(T_n = x_1^2+\cdots+x_n^2\).  
The constraints are  

\[
n\text{ odd: } S_n \le \lambda n,\qquad n\text{ even: } T_n \le n.
\]

If a player cannot choose a non‑negative \(x_n\) satisfying the constraint, the other player wins.  
The game can be drawn if it continues forever.

---

### 1.  Bazza’s winning strategy  

Assume \(\lambda < \frac{\sqrt2}{2}\).  
Bazza adopts the following strategy:

* On his first turn (\(n=2\)) he chooses \(x_2 = \sqrt2\).
* On every subsequent even turn he chooses the largest possible number so that the sum of squares exactly equals the bound:  
  \(x_{2k} = \sqrt{2k - T_{2k-1}}\) (this is always feasible because if \(T_{2k-1} > 2k\) then Alice would have won on her previous turn, which we will show never happens).

We prove by induction that after his move on turn \(2k\) we have  

\[
T_{2k}=2k,\qquad S_{2k}\ge k\sqrt2.
\]

For \(k=1\): \(S_2 = x_1+\sqrt2 \ge \sqrt2 = 1\cdot\sqrt2\) and \(T_2 = x_1^2+2 \le 2\) because Alice cannot make \(T_2 > 2\) (otherwise she would win immediately).  Hence the base holds.

Suppose the statements hold for some \(k-1\).  Alice’s move on turn \(2k-1\) is some \(x_{2k-1}\) with \(S_{2k-1}\le \lambda(2k-1)\) and \(T_{2k-1}=T_{2k-2}+x_{2k-1}^2\).  Because the game is still on, we have \(T_{2k-1}\le 2k\); otherwise Bazza would have lost on the previous turn.  Hence \(x_{2k-1}^2 \le 2\), so \(x_{2k-1}\le\sqrt2\).  Bazza then chooses \(x_{2k}=\sqrt{2k - T_{2k-1}} = \sqrt{2 - x_{2k-1}^2}\).  Then  

\[
S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + x_{2k} \ge (k-1)\sqrt2 + x_{2k-1} + \sqrt{2 - x_{2k-1}^2}.
\]

The function \(f(x)=x+\sqrt{2-x^2}\) satisfies \(f(x)\ge\sqrt2\) for every \(x\in[0,\sqrt2]\) (it attains its minimum \(\sqrt2\) at the endpoints).  Therefore  

\[
S_{2k} \ge (k-1)\sqrt2 + \sqrt2 = k\sqrt2.
\]

Moreover, \(T_{2k}=T_{2k-1}+x_{2k}^2 = (2k-2+x_{2k-1}^2)+(2-x_{2k-1}^2)=2k\).  The induction is complete.

Now Alice loses on turn \(2k+1\) when \(S_{2k} > \lambda(2k+1)\).  Because \(S_{2k}\ge k\sqrt2\), it suffices to find a \(k\) with  

\[
k\sqrt2 > \lambda(2k+1)\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad \lambda < \frac{k\sqrt2}{2k+1}.
\]

The function \(\frac{k\sqrt2}{2k+1}\) increases with \(k\) and tends to \(\frac{\sqrt2}{2}\).  Since \(\lambda < \frac{\sqrt2}{2}\), there exists a \(k\) for which \(\frac{k\sqrt2}{2k+1} > \lambda\).  For that \(k\) Alice cannot move on turn \(2k+1\), so Bazza wins.  

Thus **Bazza has a winning strategy for \(\lambda < \frac{\sqrt2}{2}\)**.

---

### 2.  Alice’s winning strategy  

Assume \(\lambda > \frac{8\sqrt2+3}{17}\).  We show that Alice can force a win.

She plays \(x_1=0\).  Bazza’s optimal response is to set \(T_2=2\), i.e. \(x_2=\sqrt2\).  Then \(S_2=\sqrt2\).  Alice sets \(x_3=0\) (playing a positive \(x_3\) would only make her future budgets smaller).  Then \(S_3=\sqrt2\), \(T_3=2\).  Bazza then sets \(T_4=4\) by taking \(x_4=\sqrt2\); hence \(S_4=2\sqrt2\).  

From this point on, Alice will always choose the maximal allowed number on her odd turns.  Define the sequence \(d_k\) by  

\[
d_1 = 5\lambda - 2\sqrt2,\qquad d_{k+1} = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2-d_k^2}\quad(k\ge1).
\]

With her strategy, after her move on turn \(2k+1\) we have  

\[
S_{2k+1} = \lambda(2k+1),\qquad T_{2k+1} = 2k + d_k^2,
\]

and then Bazza on turn \(2k+2\) sets \(x_{2k+2} = \sqrt{2-d_k^2}\) (so that \(T_{2k+2}=2k+2\)).  The budget for Alice on turn \(2k+3\) becomes  

\[
d_{k+1} = 7\lambda - S_{2k+2} = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2-d_k^2}.
\]

Thus the recurrence is correct.

Alice wins as soon as some \(d_k > \sqrt2\), because then on turn \(2k+1\) she chooses \(x_{2k+1}=d_k > \sqrt2\) and consequently  

\[
T_{2k+1} = 2k + d_k^2 > 2k+2,
\]

so Bazza cannot make a move on turn \(2k+2\) (the required \(x_{2k+2}^2\) would be negative).  

We now analyse the recurrence.  The function \(f(d)=2\lambda-\sqrt{2-d^2}\) is increasing on \([0,\sqrt2]\).  Its fixed points satisfy \(d=f(d)\), i.e.  

\[
d = \lambda \pm \sqrt{1-\lambda^2}.
\]

For \(\lambda > \frac{\sqrt2}{2}\) the upper fixed point \(d_+ = \lambda+\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\) is less than \(\sqrt2\).  Moreover, \(f(d) > d\) for \(d < d_+\) and \(f(d) < d\) for \(d > d_+\)?  Actually, because \(f\) is increasing and \(f(d_+)=d_+\), the sign of \(f(d)-d\) changes at \(d_+\).  One checks that for \(d > d_+\) we have \(f(d) > d\) (e.g. at \(d=\sqrt2\), \(f(\sqrt2)=2\lambda > \sqrt2\) when \(\lambda>\frac{\sqrt2}{2}\)).  Therefore if the initial term \(d_1\) is larger than \(d_+\), the sequence \(\{d_k\}\) is strictly increasing and will eventually exceed \(\sqrt2\); if \(d_1 \le d_+\) it stays at or below \(d_+\) and never exceeds \(\sqrt2\).

Now the condition \(d_1 > d_+\) is equivalent to  

\[
5\lambda - 2\sqrt2 > \lambda + \sqrt{1-\lambda^2}
\;\Longleftrightarrow\; 4\lambda - 2\sqrt2 > \sqrt{1-\lambda^2}.
\]

Both sides are non‑negative for \(\lambda > \frac{\sqrt2}{2}\).  Squaring gives  

\[
(4\lambda-2\sqrt2)^2 > 1-\lambda^2
\;\Longleftrightarrow\; 16\lambda^2 - 16\sqrt2\lambda +8 > 1-\lambda^2
\;\Longleftrightarrow\; 17\lambda^2 -16\sqrt2\lambda +7 > 0.
\]

The quadratic \(17\lambda^2 -16\sqrt2\lambda +7\) has roots  

\[
\lambda = \frac{16\sqrt2 \pm \sqrt{512-476}}{34} = \frac{16\sqrt2 \pm 6}{34} = \frac{8\sqrt2 \pm 3}{17}.
\]

It is positive for \(\lambda < \frac{8\sqrt2-3}{17}\) or \(\lambda > \frac{8\sqrt2+3}{17}\).  Since we are assuming \(\lambda > \frac{8\sqrt2+3}{17}\), we have \(d_1 > d_+\).  Hence the sequence \(\{d_k\}\) is strictly increasing and, as argued, must eventually exceed \(\sqrt2\).  Therefore Alice wins.

Thus **Alice has a winning strategy for \(\lambda > \frac{8\sqrt2+3}{17}\)**.

---

### 3.  The remaining values  

For \(\frac{\sqrt2}{2} \le \lambda \le \frac{8\sqrt2+3}{17}\) neither player can force a win.  Indeed, if Alice plays \(x_1=0\) and then sets \(x_{2k-1}=0\) for all \(k\ge2\), Bazza’s optimal play (always making \(T_{2k}=2k\)) results in the infinite sequence  

\[
S_{2k} = k\sqrt2,\quad T_{2k}=2k,
\]

and the inequality \(k\sqrt2 > \lambda(2k+1)\) never holds because \(\lambda \ge \frac{\sqrt2}{2}\) (the limit of the ratio is \(\frac{\sqrt2}{2}\)).  Consequently Bazza never loses, and Alice never wins.  The game can be drawn.  At the endpoints \(\lambda = \frac{\sqrt2}{2}\) and \(\lambda = \frac{8\sqrt2+3}{17}\) a draw is also the outcome (a direct computation shows that Alice cannot win when \(\lambda = \frac{8\sqrt2+3}{17}\) because the recurrence stays at the fixed point, and Bazza cannot win when \(\lambda = \frac{\sqrt2}{2}\) because the inequality fails).

---

### 4.  Conclusion  

* Alice has a winning strategy iff \(\lambda > \dfrac{8\sqrt2+3}{17}\).  
* Bazza has a winning strategy iff \(\lambda < \dfrac{\sqrt2}{2}\).  
* For \(\dfrac{\sqrt2}{2} \le \lambda \le \dfrac{8\sqrt2+3}{17}\) the game is a draw (neither player can force a win).
</solution>

- Verification that for the intermediate range a draw is possible (Alice plays all zeros, Bazza sets the squares to the bound).
